1451683405|5.0|Free Will by Sam Harris<br /><br />\\"Free Will\\" is the persuasive essay that makes the compelling case that free will is an illusion.  Free will is intuitively understood but a difficult concept to master.  Dr. Harris systematically, and with few precise words destroys the notion of the concept of free will.  With a degree in philosophy and a doctorate degree in neuroscience and the innate ability to convey difficult concepts to the layperson, Dr. Harris is best suited to enlighten us on such a challenging topic.  This 96-page book is composed of the following eight chapters:  1. The Unconscious Origins of the Will, 2. Changing the Subject, 3. Cause and Effect, 4. Choices, Efforts, Intentions, 5. Might the Truth Be Bad for Us?, 6. Moral Responsibility, 7. Politics, and 8. Conclusion.<br /><br />Positives:<br />1. Fascinating topic in the hands of a great thinker.<br />2. Profound without being unintelligible.  Elegant and accessible prose.<br />3. Does a great job of dissecting free will.  The author systematically beaks down the concept of free will by attacking it from various angles.<br />4. More so than his previous great essay \\"Lying\\" he makes more use of his scientific background.  He relays studies that support his arguments.<br />5. The illusion of being in control is a concept that Dr. Harris masterfully destroys.<br />6. The author differentiates voluntary and involuntary actions.<br />7. Great quotes, \\"Our sense of free will results from a failure to understand this: We do not know what we intend to do until the intention itself arises\\".<br />8. A discussion on the three main philosophical approaches: determinism, libertarianism, and compatibilism.<br />9. Great examples that help the reader comprehend the challenging concept of free will.<br />10. Classic Harris eloquence, \\"How can we be `free' as conscious agents if everything that we consciously intend is caused by events in our brains that we do not intend and of which we are entirely unaware? We can't\\".<br />11. Does quantum mechanics provide a foothold for free will?  Find out.<br />12. Does the process of conscious deliberation provide a foundation for free will?  Find out.<br />13. Do we really control our minds?  Once again, the mastery of Dr. Harris continues.<br />14. The implications of not having a free will. Great points!<br />15. A fascinating discussion on the level of responsibility.<br />16. How does a retributive judicial system fit in all this?<br />17. Free will within a religious framework.<br />18. Free will and politics.<br />19. A final chapter that brings everything together.<br />20. Links worked great on the Kindle.<br />21. Brief, powerful essay that can be read multiple times.<br /><br />Negatives:<br />1. My only discomfort with the essay is the casual use of the term soul.  I understand that Dr. Harris does not accept the soul as an empirical concept and may have used the term as a metaphor (equating it to the brain in one instance) but I prefer leaving out all supernatural terms unless properly defined.<br />2. Some topics are introduced briefly and leave you wanting more, isn't that always the case with Dr. Harris?<br />3. Having to wait for Dr. Harris's next intellectual contribution.<br /><br />In summary, what makes this essay great is that the more you read the more you get out of it.  It's a profound essay that is easy to follow but is hard to master.  It is so rewarding to read interesting topics from great minds.  This essay is the ultimate appetizer, delicious and with an everlasting aftertaste.  Free will is not an easy concept to understand but a worthwhile pursuit to endeavor and Dr. Harris makes the journey a fulfilling one.  I can't recommend this brief book enough, highly recommended.<br /><br />Further suggestions: \\"[[ASIN:143917122X The Moral Landscape: How Science Can Determine Human Values]]\\" by the same author, \\"[[ASIN:B005UD1EVG Who's in Charge?: Free Will and the Science of the Brain]]\\" by Michael S. Gazzaniga, \\"[[ASIN:0970818181 The Myth of Free Will, Revised & Expanded Edition]]\\" by Cris Evatt, \\"[[ASIN:0465024610 The Problem Of The Soul: Two Visions Of Mind And How To Reconcile Them]]\\" by Owen Flanagan, \\"[[ASIN:069113703X Braintrust: What Neuroscience Tells Us about Morality]]\\" by Patricia S. Churchland, \\"[[ASIN:0691154406 The Brain and the Meaning of Life]]\\" by Paul Thagard, \\"[[ASIN:0156033909 Mistakes Were Made (But Not by Me): Why We Justify Foolish Beliefs, Bad Decisions, and Hurtful Acts]])\\" by Carol Tavris, \\"[[ASIN:0521127394 Hardwired Behavior: What Neuroscience Reveals about Morality]]\\" by Lawrence Tancredi, and the \\"[[ASIN:0142003344 The Blank Slate: The Modern Denial of Human Nature]]\\" by Steven Pinker.[[ASIN:0970818181 The Myth of Free Will, Revised & Expanded Edition]]\\" by Cris Evatt, \\"[[ASIN:0465024610 The Problem Of The Soul: Two Visions Of Mind And How To Reconcile Them]]\\" by Owen Flanagan, \\"[[ASIN:069113703X Braintrust: What Neuroscience Tells Us about Morality]]\\" by Patricia S. B006ZCWTV0|5.0|Polls tell us 51% of Americans believe the bailout of the banks was wrong. An overwhelming majority --- 86% --- disapproves of big bonuses on Wall Street. And more than 50% feel that \\"the power and influence of banks and other financial institutions represent a major threat to the country.\\"<br /><br />You'd think that a lot of bands would have a song for those people.<br /><br />It's no great mystery what messages that music might deliver.<br /><br />A shout of rage for what vulture capitalists have done to workers and the dispossessed.<br /><br />A raised fist at the politicos and pundits who know exactly how the game is fixed and don't give a damn because they've got theirs.<br /><br />And a message of hope to all those who are struggling to pay their bills.<br /><br />The silence of other musicians -- well, finger-pointing no longer matters, for we now have that CD. It is fierce and blunt and angry, and unapologetic about its rage; in the Guardian's phrase, it's \\"a sledgehammer holding a megaphone.\\"<br /><br />And the guy who made it is 62.<br /><br />In Bruce Springsteen' s world, the plane leaves when he shows up. He's got acreage that a Texan might envy. His kid rides expensive horses. As he once wrote, \\"I've got the fortunes of heaven in diamonds and gold / I got all the bonds, baby, that the bank could hold.\\" If he were so inclined, he could go on a Greatest Hits tour until the end of time and end up far north of a billion dollars.<br /><br />But then he would not be Bruce Springsteen.<br /><br />If you've listened to Springsteen over the decades or even just dropped in on his seminal CDs, you know that one bright thread in his career is his concern for workers, the poor, the lost and the broken. It starts with \\"Born to Run\\" (1975). On one level, this record is a wet kiss to the American dream of endless youth and a four-lane highway with no speed limit. On another, it's a rebel yell, the defiant statement of a kid who's not going to let his soul be crushed.<br /><br />That kid grows up fast. \\"Darkness on the Edge of Town,\\" released just three years later, describes what it's like to discover you're not going to realize the dreams of your youth. The new challenge: to find dignity in defeat, to get married and have kids and still maintain some respectable definition of manhood in a system that reduces you to a cog.<br /><br />Jump-cut to \\"The Rising,\\" the 9/11 record that begged someone to make it. Not my favorite Springsteen, but it brought comfort to many, and that isn't small.<br /><br />And now \\"Wrecking Ball.\\" If we were to talk about the music here, I might say this is Springsteen's most interesting CD in a long time; you'll find jaunty Celtic melodies and gospel and rap and references to Pete Seeger and Johnny Cash. But the politics overshadows the music. As someone has said, this is the soundtrack of \\"Occupy New Jersey.\\" [<br /><br />What are the politics of \\"Wrecking Ball?\\" Simple: Springsteen hates the way people who have gained the world have lost all sense of connection to the rest of the planet. And now we --- he's in the 1%, but he identifies with the 99% --- are jobless and ignored. \\"The country we hold in our hearts is waiting,\\" Springsteen said during the 2004 election; eight years later, we are \\"shackled and drawn.\\"<br /><br />Some critics have dived into the politics of \\"Wrecking Ball,\\" and boy, that conversation can get granular fast. I admire their erudition, but I really don't think the politics is the main event here. Emotion is.<br /><br />By emotion, I mean primal feeling, what's revealed when everything you think you've got is stripped away. You can't really channel this; you need to have lived it. And Springsteen has. He's spoken of his father, \\"emasculated\\" when he lost his job in the `70s: \\"Unemployment is a really devastating thing. I know the damage it does to families. Growing up in that house, there were things you couldn't say. It was a minefield. My mother was the breadwinner. She wassteadfast and relentless and I took that from her.\\"<br /><br />In this CD, the wrecking ball that took down the old Giants Stadium becomes every dirty deal --- and every lost job:<br /><br />Now when all this steel and these stories, they drift away to rust<br />And all our youth and beauty, it's been given to the dust<br />And your game has been decided, and you're burning the down the clock<br />And all our little victories and glories have turned into parking lots<br />When your best hopes and desires are scattered to the wind<br />And hard times come, hard times go,<br />Yeah, just to come again...<br /><br />What do you do when you can't win? Just stand your ground. \\"Take your best shot,\\" he taunts the wrecking crew. And to us: \\"Hold tight to your anger/ And don't fall to your fears.\\"<br /><br />And this isn't the stance in just one song. The CD is shot through with a fierce refusal to sugarcoat bitter knowledge. Start with \\"We Take Care Of Our Own,\\" the song he did at the Grammys; only the brain dead could miss the irony.<br /><br />Then move on to \\"Rocky Ground,\\" which features this bit of rap (yes, rap):<br /><br />You use your muscle and your mind and you pray your best<br />That your best is good enough, the Lord will do the rest<br />You raise your children and you teach 'them to walk straight and sure<br />You pray that hard times, hard times, come no more<br />You try to sleep, you toss and turn, the bottom's dropping out<br />Where you once had faith now there's only doubt<br /><br />Samuel Johnson, 250 years ago, noted, \\"The natural flights of the human mind are not from pleasure to pleasure, but from hope to hope.\\" But as I hear it, the question at the heart of \\"Wrecking Ball\\" is: How can we go on when the game is rigged --- and there's no hope? That is, first, a commercial question. No one wants to listen to a 45-minute dirge --- you gotta give them something that doesn't make them want to slit their wrists. But beyond commerce, that is the emotional question that \\"Wrecking Ball\\" must address if it's to be any kind of help to us.<br /><br />And Springsteen does directly address it in what I see as the key song of the CD, \\"Land of Hope and Dreams.\\" It's not a new song; it's been a high point of his concerts for years. And deserves to be --- it's an invitation to salvation, and for everyone. Show biz theatrics? I think not. I think this is the moment when it all comes together, and the world drops away and the reason you're the one standing up there is revealed. It's a moment that chills and inspires, and it doesn't happen often enough.<br /><br />\\"Faith will be rewarded,\\" Springsteen sings in \\"Land of Hope and Dreams.\\" \\"Wrecking Ball\\" is the argument against that. And yet, against all evidence, this is what Springsteen affirms. Because although he's a fallen-away Catholic, he believes --- as I find myself believing --- that there is something bigger than we are. And that, just maybe, we'll be okay.<br /><br />A CD for the ages? Maybe not. But later, when we look back, I suspect this will be the CD that will remind us what it was like in the late winter of 2012.steadfast and relentless and I took that from her.\\"<br /><br />In this CD, the wrecking ball that took down the old Giants Stadium becomes every dirty deal --- and every lost job:<br /><br />Now when all this steel and these stories, they drift away to rust<br />And all our youth and beauty, it's been given to the dust<br />And your game has been decided, and you're burning the down the clock<br />And all our little victories and glories have turned into parking lots<br />When your best hopes and desires are scattered to the wind<br />And hard times come, hard times go,<br />Yeah, just to come again...<br /><br />What do you do when you can't win? But beyond commerce, that is the emotional question that \\"Wrecking Ball\\" must address if it's to be any kind of help to us.<br /><br />And Springsteen does directly address it in what I see as the key song of the CD, \\"Land of Hope and Dreams.\\" It's not a new song; it's been a high point of his concerts for years. And that, just maybe, we'll be okay.<br /><br />A CD for the ages? Maybe not. But later, when we look back, I suspect this will be the CD that will remind us what it was like in the late winter of 2012.

B006IDG2T6|4.0|In Free Will, the latest mini-book by Sam Harris (much like [[ASIN:B005N0KL5G Lying]]), the author is mostly preaching to the converted. I suspect that fans of his previous books, The End of Faith and Letter to a Christian Nation, will already agree that the popular notion of free will \\"cannot be made conceptually coherent.\\" I agree too, and Harris does a good (though brief) job of explaining why the \\"libertarian\\" notion of  free will (as it is called in philosophical circles) doesn't stand up to much scrutiny.<br /><br />The bigger surprise, however, is Harris' attack on compatibilism - the view that free will and determinism are compatible - which he likens to theology, saying that it \\"amounts to nothing more than an assertion to the following creed: A puppet is free as long as he loves his strings.\\" I found this section a bit lacking and unfair. Though Harris does argue his point well, mainly contending that the compatibilist notion of free will is vastly different than the popular notion of free will.<br /><br />After all, Harris certainly knows how to make a persuasive argument. He also does a great job of discussing how determinism plays into the judicial system and politics, as well as dispelling concerns about fatalism. However, at only 66 pages, it would have been nice if he'd expanded a bit more in general. But still, it was still an overall interesting book, and well worth the small investment of time.

B0028MBEFI|5.0|John Maynard Keynes (1883-1946) wrote in the Preface to this 1935 book, \\"This book is chiefly addressed to my fellow economists... its main purpose is to deal with difficult questions of theory, and only in the second place with the applications of this theory to practice... I cannot achieve my object of persuading economists to re-examine critically certain of their basic assumptions except by a highly abstract argument and also by much controversy. I wish there could have been less of the latter.\\"<br /><br />He contrasts his \\"General Theory\\" with the \\"classical economists\\" (e.g., Ricardo, Mill, Marshall, Pigou), and asserts that \\"the postulates of the classical theory are applicable to a special case only and not to the general case... Moreover, the characteristics of the special case assumed by the classical theory happen not to be those of the economic society in which we actually live... (so) that its teaching is misleading and disastrous if we attempt to apply it to the facts of experience.\\" (Pg. 3) Notably, the economic system may find itself in stable equilibrium at a level below full employment, owing to an \\"insufficiency of effective demand.\\" (Pg. 30)<br /><br />Of course, the book's most famous illustration is that if the Treasury were to \\"fill old bottles with banknotes, bury them at suitable depths... and leave it to private enterprise on well-tried principles of laissez-faire to dig the notes up again... there need be no more unemployment... It would, indeed, be more sensible to build houses and the like; but... the above would be better than nothing.\\" (Pg. 129)<br /><br />He suggests that \\"the duty of ordering the current volume of investment cannot safely be left in private hands.\\" (Pg. 320) The right remedy for the trade cycle is in \\"abolishing slumps and thus keeping us permanently in a quasi-boom.\\" (Pg. 322) He rejects, however, the idea of State Socialism; if the State simply determines the aggregate amount of resources and the basic rate of reward, \\"It will have accomplished all that is necessary.\\" (Pg. 378)<br /><br />To appreciate Keynes' work, one must note that (1) it was written during the height of the Great Depression, when unemployment was disastrous; (2) Keynes also proposed that deficit spending to stimulate demand should be REPAID during better times [a message ignored by modern politicians]; and (3) modern economic \\"stimulus\\" packages are not necessarily what Keynes would have endorsed.  Whether you agree or not, this is a seminally important work to read and study.rd, \\"It will have accomplished all that is necessary.\\" (Pg. 378)<br /><br />To appreciate Keynes' work, one must note that (1) it was written during the height of the Great Depression, when unemployment was disastrous; (2) Keynes also proposed that deficit spending to stimulate demand should be REPAID during better times [a message ignored by modern politicians]; and (3) modern economic \\"stimulus\\" packages are not necessarily what Keynes would have endorsed. B0068VZQCC|5.0|After 25 years and 9 studio albums, Blind Guardian have finally released their first 'Best Of' collection. Like all best of albums, some fans are going to quibble about the selection of songs that were picked for it and whether they truly do represent the \\"best\\" that Blind Guardian are capable of. Having said that, and with only one or two exceptions, it seems to me that the band have chosen many of the songs that fans go most crazy for at their live performances.<br /><br />With the album limited to \\"only\\" 16 tracks there also seems to be a good range of songs taken from all 9 studio albums, although it would have been nice to see ANATO represented by more than just the one track (And Then There Was Silence), even if that one track does clock in at 14 minutes! The songs on this album aren't presented in chronological order but, even with the old and new jumbled in together, there's a good flow between tracks and none seem out of place.<br /><br />The benefits of remixing the songs, and the rerecording of four of them, will also be a matter of subjective opinion amongst fans. I personally found that some of the old songs (such as Majesty and Traveler in Time) were really enhanced by the remixing process, while it wasn't quite so successful with others.<br /><br />As for the four rerecorded songs, it was interesting to hear the band revisit these songs after so many years and give them a modern touch up. If you enjoy the orchestral direction the band has taken with recent albums, then you'll more than likely also enjoy the new approach to both of the Bard's Songs. Although the originals are beloved for a reason, there are new elements to both Valhalla and And Then There Was Silence that are refreshing to hear, and I imagine there are going to be people who prefer the new version over the old and vice versa. If nothing else, these new recordings will serve as a snapshot of the present day Blind Guardian as Hansi's voice, while still being big and strong and powerful,has inevitably changed with age.<br /><br />If you're only just getting into Blind Guardian, this best of compilation is a great place to start since it includes tracks from their earliest to very latest albums and provides a nice overview of the way the band's sound has evolved over the years. On the other hand, if you're a real diehard fan then this album is worth acquiring for the liner notes alone - there are comments and reminiscences about each song and there's a wealth of candid photos that have been taken during the course of the band's career (I'm sure I'm not the only one who cracked up with laughter at the sight of Andre's Iron Maiden tshirt tucked into his checked pants... oh, 80's fashion, you were a hoot). And if you lie somewhere in between, this album may not be absolutely necessary for your collection, but it does provide variations for some of Blind Guardian's classic tunes.

While the band was always a doo-wop band that had been sniffing glue, the joy of the early records had been in the unadulterated daring of the work. This is a band that never saw boundaries and stumbled forward, bashing all the way. They never adopted arrogance or obnoxiousness, and yet the music could be the most gleefully self-destructive and rambunctious out there.<br /><br />This compilation is light on the high-gloss material, fortunately. There are some fans who will want the \\"Pet Cemetery\\" material and some who will want the material of the closing years, and those tracks are present, but most of the music is from the first three albums, and presented out of order so as to give a pretty good mix. 0792839730|4.0|\\"A Bridge Too Far,\\" (1977) is an MGM war film, a historical action drama that is based on historian Cornelius Ryan's World War II blockbuster bestselling history of the same name ([[ASIN:0684803305 A Bridge Too Far: The Classic History of the Greatest Battle of World War II]]).  It was directed by Richard Attenborough, ([[ASIN:B00003CXA4 Gandhi]]) to a script written by distinguished screenwriter William Goldman ([[ASIN:B00005M2CO Marathon Man]]).  It is fairly accurate historically, set in Holland, in 1944, based upon a joint British/American/Polish operation, dubbed Market-Garden, involving 35,000 men, in an attempt to capture several bridges to Germany.  The top brass thought that it might speed up the end of the war, to, perhaps, the famous, long-wished, home before Christmas.  It called for thousands of men to reach the area of Arnhem, by parachute, road, and even boat.  Unfortunately, the territory was inhospitable, the only roads were very narrow, hardly suited to the passage of tanks, and there were several well-rested German armies in the area about which the Dutch resistance had been trying to warn the Allies.  But the top brass didn't want to know.<br /><br />The film runs 175 minutes, and plays out on a gigantic, complicated scale showing many divisions, many armies, requiring, seemingly, many thousands of male extras.  Battle sequences are filmed onsite. They are intense and gritty, apparently, accurate, and not for the faint of heart.  Fortunately, the film is also top heavy with male stars, so that even me, who is not that knowledgeable about those things, can know, for instance, that when Michael Caine appears on screen, trotting out his best Irish accent, he's supposed to be commanding the Irish Guards.  To run through all those male stars, in no particular order whatsoever: two great favorites of mine, Caine, as Lieutenant Colonel J.O.E. Winston Churchill, Franklin D. Roosevelt, and Adolph Hitler show up as themselves, in archival footage, as do Generals Omar N. Bradley, Charles De Gaulle, Dwight D. Eisenhower, Bernard L. Montgomery, George S. Patton, and Erwin Rommel.  Joseph E. Levine got the top producers credit. Geoffrey Unsworth deserves great praise as cinematographer. The evocative, mood-setting music was by John Addison, who had himself been a tanker in the operation.<br /><br />It's really superbly done giving us fierce, chaotic glory.  Largely in color, of course, with the archival footage in black and white. The acting is topnotch throughout.  Director Attenborough must have had the abilities of a general himself, to keep all the necessary projectiles in the air. But the only two men I could recognize in SAVING PRIVATE RYAN were Tom Hanks and Matt Damon.  Of course, I can recognize most of the actors in DIRTY DOZEN.  And in this one. Even this normally reticent reviewer found himself feeling just a bit \\"shackled and drawn\\" but ready \\"to fight shoulder to shoulder and heart to heart\\" over the course of listening to this, Bruce Springsteen's most powerful work since THE RISING.<br /><br />Sincerely,<br /><br />Constant Listenere \\"trudging through the dark in a world gone wrong\\" and who literally see and feel that \\"Up on banker's hill the party's going strong/Down here below we're shackled and drawn.\\"<br /><br />It seems Springsteen's carnivalesque rock and roll-as-church motif has rarely been this black and white. B006ZCWU5K|4.0|I must admit that was with much trepidation that I looked forward to this album. The pre-release  hype about it being \\"his angriest album yet\\" filled me with both dread and curiosity, as did the fact that it was said to include \\"hip-hop\\" beats, samples, and even rap. If by hip-hop beats he means the opening  of \\"This Depression,\\"have no fear, for the drum that kicks off that song  is as old and hoary as the opening beats of J.Geil's Love Stinks (1980). Elsewhere he uses the synth-line from his own\\" Secret Garden\\" in the rousing title track, and essentially rewrites \\"All or Nothing (at All)\\"  from Human Touch on \\"You Got It\\". But there is nothing wrong with an artist of the stature and longevity of Bruce Springsteen revisiting familiar themes and motifs, and on Wrecking Ball, Bruce pays homage to every era of his long career. \\"Jack of All Trades\\" has both a haunting melody that would not have been out of place on The River, and a killer guitar solo from Tom Morello, that echoes long after the fade out. Much has been made of the \\" I would shoot the bastards on sight\\" line , but it seems to be said in a tone of bitter resignation rather than blind anger. Indeed, bitter resignation and even hope--either in the form of lyrics, or in the uplifting music that often belies the song's subject matter--seem to be the watch word, not anger. No where is this more apparent than in the jaunty Irish singalongs \\" Death to My Hometown\\", \\"Shackled and Drawn\\", and the remake of his Seeger Sessions stand out, \\"American Land\\", available as a bonus track on the Special Edition.  (Wrecking Ball will no doubt have many seeking out The Seeger Sessions, and it's Live in Dublin companion piece). Another reworked song is the fan favorite \\"Land of Hopes and Dreams\\" here making it's studio debut. It's no where near as good as the live rendition from Live in NYC, which remains definitive, but it does contain the last saxophone solo from the late Clarence Clemons, and when that sound wails in, thefinality of the Big Man's death really sinks in and it's hard for even the strongest to suppress a quiver in your lips and a tear in your eye....Wrecking Ball sounds put together, assembled. What I mean is, I sense that these songs are the fruits of many sessions over a long period of time. There are many overdubs, samples, strings, horns, voices thrown in the mix, and Ron Aniello  has done a fine job piecing it all together. One more thing about the pre-release hype. Much was made of Springsteen being influenced by the Occupy Wall Street movement. I don't believe it for one minute. There is nothing in any of these songs that capture that media-made moment. The characters in these songs are the same type of people that have populated Springsteen's songs since at least The River, and bear no similarity to the over-educated politically- charged young \\"hippies\\"  in that movement. I think Bruce -though obviously sympathetic to their aims-tried to tie in his new album to that event in an attempt to seem more revelant, more topical.  He needn't have bothered. Wrecking Ball stands on it's own as an often powerful, sometimes pedestrian album by an artist whose vision of America as a flawed experiment that often struggles to  live up to it's promise mirrors  his own career.

0545425115|5.0|Great book!  Author Suzanne Collins combines a futuristic sci-fi setting with an interesting page-turner plot which really moves to offer reading pleasure well into the night. Her novel creates a unique, richly imagined stunning fantasy world. It's a survival tale which takes place in a scientifically engineered arena of enormous proportions into which twenty- four young people aged 12-18 are placed to serve as tributes. These \\"losers\\"  are the \\"winners\\" of random selections held in their provinces along with occasional volunteers. While twenty four must compete to survive, the survival odds are extreme since the rules determine only a single victor will emerge to survive the ordeal.<br /><br />  The building blocks the author uses to create her futuristic ( and horrific) world include ideas clearly rooted in ancient traditions: spectator sports held in arenas, Olympic competitions between city-states,  people offered up as tributes to conquering nations by vanquished nations, and gladiators who survive only by winning fights to the death . Another recognizable literary theme Collins adapts is that of human prey evading and escaping human hunters. Collins makes her imaginary brave new world unique by combining these recognizable elements derived from the past with modern concepts of control:genetic engineering, environmental engineering and technical monitoring.<br /><br />  The extreme survival ordeal brings out the very best and the worst in human nature. Amidst all the horror and bloodshed,  Collins creates several very likable and very human characters who respond by revealing some wonderful character traits. Little does Kate know that she is not the only one playing \\"I've got a secret.\\"<br /><br />Pavone's characters are alive on the page and the reader becomes part of the adventure while travelling along with Kate as she tries to sift through her memories and use valuable lessons from her past to unravel the suspicious actions and underlying motivation of everyone from her husband to a couple of friends who seem to keep popping up at every turn.  One small hint to readers, the secret to truly enjoying this book will lie in your ability to keep track of the \\"today\\" scenario as the novel moves back and forth in time.<br /><br />If you happen to be a fan of the early works of Robert Ludlum this intriguing book should be moved to the top of your TBR list.  Don't be surprised if Hollywood decides that Kate Moore is the new Jason Bourne and Angelina the new Kate Moore.<br />3 1/2 stars.

One thing I do notice is that it works best with a headset with one jack and can be a little glitchy with headsets with mic/headphone dual jacks - sometimes it works with duals and sometimes it doesn't so be aware of this.<br /><br />The unit is small- about the size of a paper matchbook, is very light in weight but made of high quality material.  The surface is slippery and the clip is sturdy but slippery also so it may have trouble actually staying in place.  My advice is to clip it on the inside of a pocket so that the clip is on the outside and the unit in.  A small strip of velcro taped to a backpack pocket would make it more secure.  It charges via USB within an hour if fully drained and lasts quite a few hours of constant use.<br /><br />I highly recommend this unit if you have nerve deafness or are hard of hearing for whatever reason.<br /><br />UPDATE: June 27, 2012<br />I bought a used 8GB iPod 4th gen and some of the music files already on the iPod were transferred in low volume.  I added this amp in between the iPod and the headset and was able to hear even the faintest of songs in great volume so the only songs I took off the iPod were the ones not of my taste and not due to an inability of hearing thanks to this item!  I also bought the latest FiiO E6 to have the FiiO E5 as a backup and the E6 is better than this one due to more sound selections (i.e. more bass etc) but both work great. :)<br /><br />UPDATE: December 10, 2013<br />I still use both the E5 & E6 with my iPod 4th Gen.  They work just as they did on day one. However, I do not use them for my Samsung G3 phone due to the quirkiness of its headphone jack based on so many reviews.  I opted instead to use a Bluetooth headset with my phone.  To help with my nerve deafness, I purchased the Equalizer app from the Playstore and it acts as if I was using this physical amp.  This update doesn't exactly refer to this product but I wanted to add it for those that are hard of hearing and are finding it difficult to hear via Bluetooth headsets.

B006ZCWU5K|5.0|I think this is a really fine Springsteen album. Although I have been a fan for decades I don't think that every album is a classic, but I suspect that this may turn out to be one. The man certainly hasn't lost any of his ability to write a fine song, and there is a passion and often a rage running through Wrecking Ball which gives it real power. There is also a variety of material and styles here - country, gospel and even a little rap make an appearance - with some deeply introspective, almost Nebraska-style moments through to driving rockers like the title track, which I find makes the whole thing a riveting listen from beginning to end.<br /><br />It is the rage which strikes you most forcibly. Springsteen has always had a passionate love of his country and of its ordinary, decent people. He now also has a raw despair and fury at those who cheat those people and twist his country away from what he thinks it should and could be. This is thunderously and uncompromisingly overt in several songs - for example Jack Of All Trades (\\"If I had me a gun I'd find the bastards and shoot `em on sight\\") and Death To My Hometown:<br />\\"...Send the robber barons straight to hell -<br />Greedy thieves who came around<br />And ate the flesh of everything they found<br />Whose crimes have gone unpunished now<br />Who walk the streets as free men now<br />And brought death to my hometown.\\"<br /><br />Powerful stuff, and a great song which is sung with a fury and blame very different from the sad, fatalistic acceptance of economic hardship in songs like The River and My Hometown in the 80s. There is more on this album than just rage, though. Springsteen's strength as a songwriter has always been his ability to write a good, straightforward tune and to convey important human experiences and truths through singing about the small specifics of life, especially working life. He does a great job of that on this album and it is also good to see that he can still write a fine, direct love/lust song in You Got It.<br /><br />That great voice is still just as great, and the production is, as always, excellent. There is, of course, one giant absentee and I found the huge gap left by Clarence Clemons yawned at me on occasions, despite a fine brass section. Clarence makes his only appearance on Land Of Hope And Dreams - Track 10 - and to hear him suddenly so far through the album was almost unbearably poignant. It is a great tribute to a man whose sound I have loved and which has followed me down three decades and more.<br /><br />I warmly recommend this album, especially to those who may be wondering whether they really need another Springsteen album. 1451683405|5.0|While many philosophers (and people in general) dodge this \\"touchy subject,\\" Sam Harris tells it like it is.  And he does so in his signature style, plowing through the philosophical mumbo-jumbo in a direct, succinct manner.  It's easy for the layman to understand and can be read in an hour or two.<br /><br />This book will be a most refreshing read to those who are sick and tired of hearing the sayings \\"there's no excuse for your poor choices\\" or \\"you make your own luck\\" or \\"take some responsibility for your choices.\\"  While such sayings can be useful as motivational tools or helpful in causing one to change his behavior, ultimately we are never in a position to explain why such a change actually takes place (or fails to take place).  Harris describes why this is the case by showing how what seems like our ability to make \\"conscious choices\\" is actually just our awareness of choices that have been made unconsciously.  We experience an illusion of beeing the source of these choices but we never actually are that source.<br /><br />While some readers will initially (and mistakenly) interpret Harris' description as an endorsement of nihilism or fatalism, Harris takes a chapter to describe why deliberation, choices, intentions, hopes, fears, etc. are still as important as advocates of free will believe them to be.  Contrary to popular opinion, \\"no free will\\" need not lead to \\"meaninglessness\\" or \\"no morals.\\"  Harris writes how he believes that his acceptance of no free will has actually made him a better person, in that it's given him greater compassion for those who've been dealt a bad hand in life and humility in recognizing just how good a hand he's been dealt.

B002SSUQFG|4.0|This watch is my first (and only) \\"Automatic\\" winding watch.  I've never been good at wearing watches.  What I mean is that I usually end up cracking the lenses, breaking the bands, or simply losing them.  And, the only reason I bought this watch is because it was a requirement for EMT school.  (The teacher insisted that we have a watch that clearly showed the seconds, which supposedly would make taking pulse rates easier.  Go figure.)  So, after searching Amazon and reading reviews, I decided to buy this one.  It seemed to be the light-weight, low-profile, relatively inexpensive watch that I was looking for.  For the purposes I've listed, I'd recommend this watch, but that recommedation would not be without some qualifiers.  To make things simple, I'll list the good and the bad:<br /><br />The Good:<br />-Narrow band and small watch face makes for more comfortable wearing.  (Easier to bend wrist while wearing it. Easy to position higher or lower on wrist.)<br />-Unlike metal bands, this one doesn't pinch or pull hairs.<br />-Unlike rubber bands, this one doesn't cause sweating.<br />-Lens has been remarkably wear resistant.  (As a firefighter/EMT, I've banged this watch against hard/sharp objects hundreds of times now, thinking for certain that I would have scratched or cracked it.  So far, there's not a single scratch!)<br />-Band snaps free of watch when caught on an object. (I've snagged the watch several times now on different objects during work which resulted the band snapping loose from the watch body.  The reason I've listed this as \\"Good\\" is because each time it has been very easy to simply pop the band's retaining, spring-rods (don't know the technical term) back into the watch.  So, its good that the watch will separate/snap free when snagged without causing any permanent damage the band retainers!)<br />-If worn consistently (every day for a several hours each day), the watch doesn't require manual winding.<br />-Shows the seconds as the largest numbers, making it very easy to count off seconds.<br />-Water resistance seems to be well designed.  (Its been sprayed with pressurized water and submerged and so far shows no signs of leaks.)<br />-Back of watch is transparent, allowing one to see the inner workings.  Very cool!<br /><br />The Bad:<br /><br />-Does not stay wound very long if not worn constantly.  If you skip a day wearing this watch (or if you wear it but don't move around a lot, it will wind down over night and necessitate resetting it.<br />-Tricky to get it to keep time accurately.  At first, I decided to do the manual winding method (shake it from side to side) every morning on each day I would wear it, but that resulted in it being over-wound.  So, it would keep time too fast or gain time (approx. 10 mins within a couple days).  Then, I decided to not do any manual winding, but quickly discovered that it would keep time too slowly or loose time (approx. 10 mins per 2 days).  Ultimately, there's a sweet spot where one learns just the right ratio of manual winding to \\"automatic\\" winding.  At this point in ownership, I've managed to establish a manual/automatic winding routine where at most I'll gain or lose only about 2 to 3 minutes from actual time over the course of a week.  And, according to what I've read, a 2 to 3 minute variance in 7 days is very good accuracy for a mechanical watch in this price range.<br />-Can be annoying to set the day of the week and calendar day.  (There's a specific time window each day that you can directly set these.  It has to do with the A.M. and P.M. positioning.  Since there's no visual indicator of whether the watch is on the rise or fall of the 12/24 hour clock cycle, you can't necessarily simply change the day of the week and calendar day to what it's supposed to be and expect it to change correctly on the next day.  You may have to also change the time, cycling through an entire 12 hour interval, to make sure that the date will change at midnight instead of noon.  I hope that makes sense...)<br />-Glow-in-the-dark watch hands are not very bright or of long duration.  (If they're exposed to a bright light (sunlight or intense, up close lamp light), the hands glow brightly in the dark, but only for a few minutes.  I know that all such phosphorescent-based photoluminescent materials are subject to gradual fading, but the hands on this watch seem to be the fastest-fading, dimmest of any watch I've ever owned. This is very problematic if you need to check the time or count seconds when its dark.<br /><br />Conclusion:<br /><br />Despite the problems listed above, I'm mostly satified so far with the performance of this watch.  Once I worked out/adapted to its quirks, it serves me well.  I'm especially impressed with its durability.  I've put this watch through some heavy abuse, but it has remained scratch and defect free!  As someone who has always hated wearing watches, I've grown to like wearing this one so much that I'm thinking of buying another with a brown band to wear to church and such, since my current black band clashes with most of my dress clothes/shoes/coats.

B006ZCWTV0|4.0|Wrecking Ball is an angry album dealing with hard and desperate times: unemployment, economic discrepancies, and personal displacement are just a few of the underlying themes addressed.  It is also an album where many of the musical styles Bruce Springsteen has engaged in come together, along with new elements such as loops and a more pronounced use of female singers.<br /><br />It opens with We Take Care of Our Own, a song that musically sounds like vintage E Street Band; it is, in its own way, as powerful an opening track as Badlands or Born in the U.S.A.  Like that latter song, it could receive a mistaken interpretation by the casual listener drawn in to the catchy chorus.  But, where the chorus declares \\"We take care of our own,\\" the lyrics examine an America where needed help never appears.<br /><br />Shackled and Drawn and Death to My Hometown both bear strong resemblances to the tracks Springsteen performed during his Seeger Sessions time.  With their Irish feel, they sound like songs that Shane MacGowan could sink his crooked teeth into with joy.  Easy Money, a song about a man going out with his lover to commit crimes to make some cash, has a ramshackle, country feel that perfectly matches Springsteen's grizzled snarl.<br /><br />The title track presents Springsteen reminiscing about coming up in the \\"swamps of Jersey,\\" referencing his classic track Rosalita.  It is a defiant song in which Bruce dares all comers to \\"take your best shot/let me see what you got.\\"  It is an exhilarating song; at 62, The Boss is still willing to throw down the gauntlet.<br /><br />Wrecking Ball, for all its anger, ends on a hopeful note.  Land of Hope and Dreams, a song that debuted during the 1999-2000 E Street reunion tour, uses the imagery of the freedom train carrying passengers to a better destination.  The final track (on the regular edition release), We Are Alive, with sweeping, loping music that could fit in a 60s western, tells of departed souls rising up in solidarityand strength.  One final note: midway through Land of Hope and Dreams, we hear a saxophone solo by the late Clarence Clemons.  It's like receiving a call from a long lost friend.  For a few moments, it seems as if the Big Man is truly as immortal as implied by Springsteen in his moving eulogy.  It is a transcendent moment, one of the high points of a truly fine addition to the Springsteen canon.  Recommended listening.

B000WQBO8O|5.0|Review of the 12\\" vinyl original of Santana's 1972 album 'Caravanserai'<br /><br />Released in October 1972, `Caravanserai' marks one of Santana's highest and most sublime creative moments and has often been described as \\"music for musicians\\". It's the last of Santana's studio recordings to feature founder-member Gregg Rolie on keyboards and second-guitarist (the very young but virtuoso) Neal Schon, both of whom were shortly to depart to form `Journey.' Rolie features on some tracks and new keyboard player Tom Coster appears on others, the start of a decades-long and highly productive association between Coster and Santana.<br /><br />Founder-member David Brown is replaced on bass by Doug Rauch (on some of the numbers) and Tom Rutley (on others) and the departure of Mike Carabello as conga-percussionist heralded the entry of veteran Armando Peraza - who, I can tell you from seeing this band live in 1973, had one heck of an onstage presence and added a lot to the sound. Drummer Mike Shrieve and percussionist `Chepito' are still there, both evident in the music. Altogether `Caravanserai' showcases contributions by some 16 different musicians and is a masterpiece of arrangement and production; it marks the beginning of a rich and productive journey into deeper, more complex jazz-fusion territory which continued with `Welcome' and - especially - `Borboletta.'<br /><br />The trademark rock-salsa fusion sound which made Santana's first three studio albums a global commercial success gives way here to more thoughtful, jazz-like compositions, though you can hear the genealogy of the Woodstock-era band still there underneath. Of the 10 numbers on the album, only three have any vocal content and the first six pieces (i.e. the first `side' of the original vinyl LP) flow together as one, with no real breaks. `Caravanserai' though impressive on first listening, is not such an instantly-accessible musical listening experience as the first three albums. The band experiments with different time-signatures and instrumentation in complex compositions to weave an engrossing musical tapestry which wins over the head, the heart and the soul where repeat-listening reveals ever more depth and subtlety.<br /><br />If you like to seek out great music of any era and especially if you have a penchant for jazz-rock fusion and you've never heard `Caravanserai', then you're in for a rare treat. This mid-1970s period was the high-point of Carlos Santana's long creative career: here he is in his prime, a master of his craft with experience and global success behind him, but wanting to explore music a bit deeper, maybe with less popular appeal but ultimately more personally satisfying, more valuable and enduring. Put on the headphones, lie back, crank up the volume and be transported to a land rich, beautiful and sublime.<br /><br />I am BTW writing this in Hatta, close to the UAE/Oman border. As the Sun rises, a herd of wild camels is visible on a distant hill to the South: a real-life spectacle almost identical to the original cover-art of this: Santana's great, timeless fourth album.ifferent time-signatures and instrumentation in complex compositions to weave an engrossing musical tapestry which wins over the head, the heart and the soul where repeat-listening reveals ever more depth and subtlety.<br /><br />If you like to seek out great music of any era and especially if you have a penchant for jazz-rock fusion and you've never heard `Caravanserai', then you're in for a rare treat. This mid-1970s period was the high-point of Carlos Santana's long creative career: here he is in his prime, a master of his craft with experience and global success behind him, but wanting to explore music a bit deeper, maybe with less popular appeal but ultimately more personally satisfying, more valuable and enduring. This Dragon is a powerful and cunning monster - it hides its real form (and it can even disguise its real voice), it doesn't feel pain, it never sleeps, it doesn't need to eat, drink or even rest, it knows no mercy, no fear, no doubt - and as if it was not enough, all its vital organs are covered with nearly indestructible armor... This Knight, who looks like Michael Biehn, is still young, but in the same time he is already a precociously aged battle scarred veteran, who slayed many a lesser dragon before, but who never yet faced such a formidable foe as this particular Beast. 0743442539|5.0|Is this a spy novel? Yes it is.<br /><br />Does that tell you what the book is like? Not really.<br /><br />I read this book today, almost five decades after it was written and it is very impressive. When I think of a cold war era spy novel I think of books full of details about the mechanics of espionage, books which impress by their knowledge of how the game is played, books which contain a thrill a minute and are full of three-letter acronyms like KGB, CIA and MI6.<br /><br />For this book though, that whole world of spy-craft and suspense seems incidental rather than central, just a context in which Le Carre places a story that is so well written that it is more about human frailties than it is about spies. In fact, the author seems reluctant to side with any one ideology, preferring to focus instead on the moral bankruptcy which the greyness and extreme result-orientedness of this world leads to.<br /><br />John Le Carre writes with a sensitivity which one would expect in an intellectual love story or a literary masterpiece, not a spy thriller. Every character is deftly painted with a little mannerism or the tone of a statement revealing something about them. It's almost as if every scene is written with a focus on its characters and not on its events. When Le Carre makes the plot take a twist, we don't marvel at the cleverness of the writer, we feel the dismay of the protagonist whose premises have just been shaken up. It is this quality of the book which makes every sentence engrossing.<br /><br />Make no mistake though, this is a spy novel with an intricate plot. While the quality of the writing will make you hang on to every word, the raciness of the narrative will  make you want to turn the pages quickly. At the end, it will leave you feeling empathy with its characters (almost all of them, on both sides of the fence) rather than identifying with the winner.

B005MMJ84K|3.0|SPOILERS AHEAD!!!!<br /><br />Like so many other reviewers, Delirium was one of my favorite novels of 2011 and I couldn't wait for 2012 when Pandemonium would come out. Delirium spends the whole novel getting the reader invested in Lena, her transformation, and her falling in love with Alex. The end had me dying for the next book so I could see what happened to Lena and Alex.<br /><br />Pandemonium opens with Lena escaping in the Wilds, leaving Alex behind, presuming him dead. She stumbles upon a group of the resistance and integrates into their society. Unlike many of the other reviewers, I like the \\"Then\\" segments. I thought that it was necessary to show the completion of Lena's transformation from \\"Zombie\\" to the hard, practical, strong member of the resistance she becomes. It is also the only time we really see her grieve for Alex.<br /><br />The \\"Now\\" segments follow Lena as she is sent on a mission to follow the prodigal son, Julian, of the Pro-Cure leader. This irked me because there is obviously something going on but Lena doesn't question it or push the matter. I found the fact that her resistance \\"mentors,\\" Raven and Tack, are a part of a scheme that paid Scavengers to abduct Lena and Julian is ludicrous. I find it hard to believe that they would employ the aid of a faction that is so reviled and hated by the resistance. Plus, Raven and Tack left a great deal up to chance. I found it unbelievable that Lena was able to A) make her way to Julian through the crowds, B) keep track of him once she had found him, C) find him once he had been abducted, and D) manage to make it to him without becoming a casualty herself. I thought it was unnecessary that Raven and Tack kept her in the dark when she could have performed her task so much better with more knowledge. They didn't even have to reveal everything. It could have been \\"We heard a rumor that Julian would be abducted by the Scavengers at the rally - keep an eye on him\\" and we could have still had the OMG moment later on when we discover that the resistance paid the Scavengers. And it was irritating that Lena spent so much of the novel passive, helpless, dirty, and/or passed out. We were treated to a few scenes, but I wanted to see more of the fighter she had become!<br /><br />It was entirely predictable that Lena would fall for Julian. \\"Oh my God, we're stuck in a room together and I hate you and everything you stand for but your whining and naivete are so attractive that I can't help myself.\\" I didn't connect with Julian at all. I hated that I constantly felt sorry for him because of his father, his brother, his cancer, etc. As a reader, I don't want to pity the male love interest in a novel. I especially didn't connect with his and Lena's \\"love.\\" She flipped from one extreme to the other so quickly and he just seemed like a horny teenage boy who got to touch a girl for the first time. (BTW, his peeping while she took a shower didn't seem sexy to me, it was creepy.) Everything between them felt forced and contrived. Maybe it was because I was so invested in Lena and Alex's relationship that this book just didn't do it for me.<br /><br />Now, I don't know about you, but from watching a lot of TV and reading a lot, I KNOW that when a character \\"dies\\" - unless you see the person die and then witness them being buried or burned - you can pretty much guarantee that there's a good chance you'll be seeing them again. That for me was predictable and I was not at all surprised when Alex showed up on the last page, interrupting a lovey-dovey moment between Julian and Lena. I am so tired of the teenage love-triangle in YA novels. Just because it worked in Hunger Games doesn't mean it's the best plot direction for this series. While I loved Delirium, and this series had the potential to become the next HG for me, this second book ruined it by forcing a triangle. I feel like this book would have been better without the love-triangle set up.<br /><br />However, with all that said,I *will* be reading the third book to find out what happens with Lena and Alex and to see if, hopefully, it can redeem the disappointment of this book. :(

B004LWZW7E|3.0|In Time presents an abnormal futuristic society where time is the newest currency of choice.  Enter Will Salas (played by Justin Timberlake), destined to live a life of little means, meaning he lives to gain more time, working, day in and day out, to prolong his own life and that of his mother, Rachel, played by Olivia Wilde.  If you're wondering about Olivia Wilde playing his mother, let me break things down for you.  Everyone physically stops aging at 25.  They have been genetically engineered not only to stop aging at 25 but also have an internal clock (okay, well, it's not so internal, because the countdown is visible on everyone's arm) that is counting down until the moment they die (and when they do die, it looks like they have some kind of massive heart attack, so maybe it's actually some internal bomb that destroys the heart, which, hey, since we don't get any explanations, could actually be mechanical).  In this way, it is possible for people to live hundreds of years, thus looking 25 but actually being around 38 and so on.  The catch is that they have to keep earning time by working (and lose it when making purchases, like buying food or wanting to take a bus).  When a person's clock reaches zero, they die.  The poor struggle for more time (little side conversations are dropped here and there about needing to hurry), while the rich are born and destined to live a long, long time.<br /><br />Everything changes the day Will encounters a rich and reckless Henry Hamilton (Matt Bomer) who flashes his many years in one of the poorest sections of town, an act which incites trouble (keep a lookout for some futuristic gangsters, led by Fortis, played by Alex Pettyfer, and one of his cronies who dresses ridiculously - that hat annoyed me to death - he's supposed to look tough?) and sets this tale in motion.  Will saves his life, Henry reveals that he has lived a very long time and is ready for it all to end (he also drops some information that shocks Will but whichisn't all that shocking to the viewer), and by morning, Henry is gone, and Will has more years than he has ever dreamed of earning.  Shortly after, a tragedy happens and Will is determined to get revenge.<br /><br />While I think this movie is interesting, you have to suspend your disbelief to enjoy it.  You're told that people stop aging at 25 due to genetic engineering, and then their countdown begins (which makes it possible to live forever if characters play their cards right)...but this isn't delved into too deeply.  The subject of health problems is never brought up, so I only assume that this is such an advanced society that even cancer has been eradicated (as no one in the movie is sick; also, no one is overweight, so I guess they've taken care of obesity as well).  Along with this, the entire subject of the genetic engineering that has led to this current state of affairs is mentioned the one time and never talked about again.  Characters also exchange time (which confused the heck out of me when the film began).  They hold onto each other or use a little metal device and can take and give time (again, this is never explained and the viewer is left to speculate how this is possible).  In Time doesn't explain things.  It doesn't explain what happened to illnesses, how aging was stopped, how this genetic engineering thing works (or even how it was accomplished), how people can physically exchange time in order to gain more or give their own freely, and even how time can be used as a currency.  I think these oversights (but hey, they might have just messed the movie up even more if they had been included, maybe leading to more confusion), along with bad casting (which is covered next), are the things which ruin this movie.<br /><br />I know Justin Timberlake mainly as a singer, and, while, I'm not against singers turned actors, I wasn't overly moved by his performance or even attached to his character.  I don't think he did an awful job, but it wasn't anythingnoteworthy, either.  On a similar note, Amanda Seyfried as the spoiled Sylvia Weis (who becomes the Bonnie to Will's Clyde) manages to make herself useful only by standing around, her eyes looking gigantic and catlike, her wig very fake, and her actions very robotic; oh, and let's not forget how smart it is to wear a pair of high heels (because being stylish when you're being naughty is oh, so intelligent; and you definitely won't have to run when being on the country's most wanted list - *please note the heavy sarcasm).  On the one hand, I find the concept of time as currency intriguing, but the movie drags on and on, the acting isn't the best (though Olivia Wilde and Cillian Murphy stand out), the amount of unanswered questions I had about this society grew and grew as the movie progressed, and there was a certain inconsistancy to some of the characters.<br /><br />Tragedy takes place, Will crosses the border separating the rich from the poor, and his sadness over his loss seems to vanish (barring instances where he's moodily staring off into space, all the while - of course - being watched by Sylvia and her massive eyes).  I was enjoying the movie before he went into the richer portion of town.  Once he went there, it was apparent that this movie was going to shirk the loss this character had undergone, and throw a lot out all for the sake of a romance with no spark (and a heroine whose sudden change in opinions felt rushed and out of character).  The first scenes between Will and Sylvia were annoying and didn't go with what had already taken place in the film.<br /><br />Later, Will and Sylvia take action and start behaving like a futuristic Robin Hood/Maid Marian pairing, stealing from the poor to give to the rich.  I thought this was a little interesting, but it wasn't enough.  Cillian Murphy's character had so much potential, not only in the development in his character but because he can actually act.  Unfortunately, I think what happens with this characterwas too predictable.  It was the easy way to end things, and it was also a cop-out.<br /><br />On a more positive note, I liked the futuristic police cars, the concept of the movie was interesting (though the message behind things was overly stressed), and I enjoyed certain scenes.  I've considered whether I think this is worth a 2.5 (not possible on Amazon) or 3 star rating, and I'm going to have to go with a 3-star rating.  I liked some things about the movie.  It's very different and quite interesting, but the acting doesn't hold up (and I honestly think the inclusion of Sylvia's character is one of the downfalls of the film), there are numerous plot holes and unanswered questions, and it's more of a sit-back-don't-think-and-just-enjoy type flick.  Don't expect too much from this.<br /><br />Overall: This movie definitely won't appeal to everyone.  I think the cons largely outweigh the pros in this case, but I sort of enjoyed watching it (sort of).  This wasn't a film I hated, and I didn't love it either.

B003L20IFQ|3.0|Paul Conroy, a private contractor, working as a truck driver in Iraq, wakes up after an ambush to discover he is inside a wooden coffin buried beneath the desert sand. He has nothing but a lighter, a flask with whiskey, some pills and a cell phone provided to him by his captors. He begins to make desperate calls for help as time drags on and his situation becomes more and more dire.<br /><br />This is the premise (and a large portion of the plot) of BURIED, an independent film from Spain, starring Ryan Reynolds in what is essentially a one-man show. It's a tense little thriller that is nearly spoiled by some serious flaws. Reynolds gives a fairly convincing performance as a fairly ordinary guy who begins his ordeal in a panic and only gradually finds his way to calm. At least, he's calm for awhile.<br /><br />The movie is mostly the various conversations he has with the outside world. Grappling with lost signals (and a dying battery), answering machines and a disbelieving bureaucracy. He can't reach his loved ones. He can't convince his captors that he doesn't have $5 million dollars to exchange for his freedom. There's really nothing he can do about his situation...he has no way to break free and must rely entirely on a world of people who aren't quite as motivated as he is.<br /><br />Director Rodrigo Cortes does a good job of finding as many ways of filming the events of the films as he can. His camera swoops around inside the coffin, yet we always have the claustrophobic feeling. The film has a simple premise (sort of in the way that OPEN WATER was a simple, claustrophobic little film), but Cortes and company grab all they can out of it.<br /><br />However, even though we can believe that Conroy would frequently panic and make some bad decisions...he makes too many bad choices. When he does reach people on the phone, he does an unbelievably bad job of convincing them that he really needs their help. I can believe him losing his cool once or twice...but he does it fairly consistently. And when he finally DOES reach someone who might be willing to help, he spends a lot of time sharing unneeded information. He knows his battery is running down, yet he often wastes his breath. It got to the point where I occasionally thought, \\"Well, if he dies, he has no one to blame but himself.\\" (Notwithstanding that he didn't put himself in the coffin.)<br /><br />Worst of all, though...the film manages to take some pretty nasty jabs at the mission in Iraq and make some attempts at moral relativism. One of the folks working to help Conroy, a hostage expert in Iraq, actually tries to explain away the terrorist acts as the work of people who will simply do anything to survive. \\"You'd do the same if you were in their situation, Paul,\\" this awful man says. REALLY??? If a time comes when the only way I can think to feed my family is to kidnap folks and bury them alive...go ahead and shoot me. To dare suggest that Americans would be just as bad as these terrorist extremists is a mighty big (and offensive) argument for a little thriller to make. For unsubtle, unearned and unsubstantiated political/social statements inserting themselves where they don't belong, BURIED takes the cake.<br /><br />The day after seeing the film, what I remember most is not the tension and excitement and fear...but the bad taste in my mouth from the group of Spanish filmmakers and producers who took pot shots at America.<br /><br />I mention all this, because the film is generally very effective (though not as tense as some other reviews I've read have implied). If you don't mind the politics I mention, by all means enjoy the film. If you find the premise intriguing enough that you think you need to overlook the politics...then you'll have a good time. I just wish someone had warned me.

B0067Q04AM|4.0|I am surprised by the incredibly high praise this album is receiving. It's certainly a very good album, just not quite a great one. I have read a number of reviews comparing the project to Miles Davis which is a little ridiculous to me. I've recently gotten into jazz a bit, and Miles Davis' \\"In A Silent Way,\\" \\"Bit@%es Brew\\" or \\"On the Corner\\" are leagues ahead of the sound on this album. But, they are also less accessible than Robert Glapser Experiment's more straightforward, Contemporary R&B influenced style. Though no one has combined genres in quite the way this project has, the tracks on \\"Black Radio\\" end up having a familiar sound. They are really not particularly experimental if you have explored neo-soul, a genre which has been strongly influenced by jazz and hip-hop since its beginnings. Rather than truly pushing the boundaries of the genre, it sticks close to conventions. Similar instrumentation is utilized on most tracks, with the majority being mellow songs relating to relationships. Aside from an arguable lack of innovation, the songs here are generally successful, as most are very well executed. However, the album is not without its faults. It is difficult to excuse a few significant disappointments on the album such as Yasiin Bey's (Mos Def's) limited role on the track \\"Black Radio\\", some silly lyrics by Lupe Fiasco (Ninja Turtles?) on \\"Always Shine,\\" and the droning drum beat introduced near the beginning of the Nirvana \\"Smells Like Teen Spirit\\" cover (though I did find the song to be an interesting contribution to the album). If you enjoy mature neo-soul and R&B you will probably enjoy this, as I did. But depending on how adventurous you are in seeking out music outside of the mainstream, you may not find it particularly unique. I find myself more engaged by the RH factor's 2003 album \\"Hard Groove\\" which was based on a similar concept. With some hesitance, due to the overall quality of the project, I can give \\"Black Radio\\" four stars.<br /><br />Favourite tracks:<br />Afro Blue (Featuring Erykah Badu)<br />Cherish the Day (Featuring Lalah Hathaway)<br />Move Love (Featuring King)<br />The Consequences of Jealousy (Featuring Me'Shell Ndegeocello)<br />Why Do We Try (Featuring Stokely Williams)<br />Letter to Hermione (Featuring Bilal)

B002G1YPH0|5.0|I've seen many reviews for this item, and it has to be one of the most popular heatsinks sold. I don't know what I can say that others haven't already said. Some gripe about the size. It's pretty standard these days. 120mm fans are the same size no matter which heatsink they are on. I've owned several heatsinks, and this one has to be the one of the easiest to install. I'm replacing a whiny Zalman 9700 NT with this one, which I liked Zalman's mounting ring with backplate design too. I had to remove the mobo anyway to install this. The instructions are kind of confusing. I followed the pictures and found its really easy. Bracket on one side of the mobo. Push through the bolts and screw on the nuts. Place HSF on top of chip and screw down retention bracket in alternating pattern. Done. Love this design. I absolutely hate the Intel push-pin design on others. I've seen it bow motherboards.<br /><br />Backplate seems unobtrusive enough that it doesn't interfere with components on the back of the mobo. I had a problem with another model with a universal backplate that I was trying to mount on a EVGA 780i FTW mobo. The backplate hit the NB heatsink mount on the back and prevented it from sitting correctly.<br /><br />People have mentioned memory clearance issues. Must be on AMD based boards. I have it mounted on a Gigabyte GA-EP45-UD3P with no issues. It's even in a crappy cheapo case with the PSU at the top and the HSF clears everything with 1/8\\" to spare. In my AMD rig, I have a Xigmatek S1283 that is very similar to this one. I have to use low profile memory otherwise it hits the bottom of the aluminum radiator. As it is, it prevents me from removing memory from 2 slots. HSF mounts 90 degrees clockwise on a AMD socket compared to Intel. Xigmatek used the dreaded push-pin design for this, luckily I am using it for AMD, so it still uses the stock clipping system. I think its possible to rotate this so that the HSF is oriented towards the back, though I haven't been able to test this. Not true of the S1283, unfortunately.<br /><br />How well does it work? Great! I was running 45C+ on full load with a Q9550 @ 2.83Ghz with the Zalman 9700. Single fan brought it down to the low 30s. I've added a second fan and brought the Q9550 up to 3Ghz. 39C full load, 26C idle. Impressive for air!<br /><br />Lastly, I liked it so much I bought a second for another project I am working on.

B006ZCWU5K|3.0|\\"Wrecking Ball,\\" Bruce Springsteen's first record in three years, has been met with considerable anticipation by fans over the previous months. Described in various interviews as his `angriest' record to date, as well as his most diverse and experimental sounding in years, many fans were hoping for another top shelf effort from The Boss.  Unfortunately, despite his best efforts, \\"Wrecking Ball\\" doesn't reach the lofty heights many had hoped for.<br /><br />It's not that it's bad. The record is a definite improvement over 2009's lazy \\"Working on a Dream\\" (\\"Queen of the Supermarket?\\"), and there's a lot to commend about it. The most striking compliment that can be given is to produer Ron Aniello, who replaces Brendan O'Brien at the helm. Aniello gives the songs the kind of breathing room that O'Brien never was able to achieve; the compressed, ear splitting loudness of \\"Magic\\" and \\"The Rising\\" are now replaced by a smoother, better separated sound that lets each instrument shine and allows for the more subtle touches to be better appreciated.<br /><br />The record also lives up to its promise of being `experimental.' Musically, it's an excellent record, and there is really no Springsteen album that sounds like it; part Seeger Sessions, part traditional E Street, with touches of soul, R & B and even mariachi and rap popping up at various points on the record.<br /><br />However, the record's weakness lies in its most crucial factor: Bruce.  For all the effort put into getting the arrangements, sound and production bang-on, there is comparatively little effort put into the actual words of the songs. There are no lyrics on this record as moving, precise or detailed as, say, the last verse of \\"The River,\\" the opening of \\"Thunder Road,\\" or to use a more recent example, the last verse of \\"Long Walk Home.\\"  The lyrics range from being banal and simplistic (the clich&eacute; laden first single \\"We Take Care of Our Own\\" and the list of chores that is \\"Jack of All Trades\\") to downright embarrassing (\\"Easy Money,\\" the novelty title song). The songs are impeccably dressed up with no place to go.<br /><br />There are some strong moments to be sure. \\"Shackled and Drawn\\" and \\"Death to My Hometown\\" are both powerful, Celtic tinged rave-ups, \\"You've Got It\\" is jarring thematically but is a nice roadhouse blues, and the sample laden \\"Rocky Ground\\" is a bit repetitive but works well, and even the rap towards the end (not by Bruce but by a female singer whose name escapes me at the moment) is surprisingly effective.  Best of the brand new ones is \\"We Are Alive,\\" a tribute to fallen souls lost to the strains of inequality and social repression loaded with energy and spirit.<br /><br />The album's best overall moment, somewhat unfortunately, is the thirteen year old \\"Land of Hope and Dreams,\\" debuted in 1999 on the E Street Band's Reunion Tour. The studio take of the song takes the passion of the live performances to a whole new level, and is also significant for featuring the final recorded appearance of Clarence Clemons, who passed away last June.<br /><br />The deluxe edition of the record comes with two bonus songs, a studio take on the Seeger Sessions gem \\"American Land\\" and Leonard Cohen / Tom Waits esque \\"Swallowed Up (In the Belly of a Whale).  \\"American Land\\" gets a heavier, less folksier E Street Band feel, and it works well, giving it a Dropkick Murphys vibe that it previously lacked.  \\"Swallowed Up\\" is a sparse, haunting acoustic number with apocalyptic undertones that stands as one of the most pleasantly unusual numbers in Bruce's ouvre.<br /><br />While \\"Wrecking Ball\\" has quite a few highlights and shows that The Boss is still willing to branch into different directions musically, it also sadly represents a decline in Bruce's abilities as a lyricist. Gone are the great stories and characters that listeners can close their eyes and envision. It's now simply buzz words and stock imagery that does not paint as nearly as compelling a picture.

B006UG90RM|5.0|Lifelong VH fan and completely objective in terms of albums/eras... fan of everything from Simmons demo tape to even VH3 (well, not much to love from that one, but Eddie was in a bad place at that time; Gary Cherone is a great vocalist though). I'm a fan of VH like I'm a fan of James Bond... i.e., find the positive in the unique characteristics of each period, if you will. If you're a rock/hard rock fan, and especially a VH fan, and don't love this album, you should probably go back to listening today's current Top 40 and stick with Bieber!<br /><br />I've read so-called music critics question and debate everything with the release of Different Kind of Truth (DKOT) such as the album's title, Eddie's sobriety/appearance, to Wolfie's playing and vocals compared to Michael Anthony, to Dave's vocal range and on-stage theatrics (or lack thereof), they used some old, original and unreleased music, there are too many tracks on the album (who complains about getting more for their money these days?!?), etc... etc... give me (and the band) a break already! At the end of the day, it's all petty. You don't listen to and/or attend the concert of an iconic rock band to see their dance moves or dress, or debate the \\"politics\\" of rock - you have a good time and get loud with one of the best ever. If this album had been released by a \\"new\\" band, instead of one coming back, people would probably be saying \\"Welcome the new kings of rock.\\" Instead, it's \\"Welcome back one of the kings of rock.\\"<br /><br />And DKOT delivers! I listened to this album multiple times, front to back, on a recent road trip and can honestly say there isn't a track on this album that doesn't kick tail. There is at least one moment on almost all of the tracks where you just replay it louder, and louder - and say \\"Man, that is just in your face right there.\\"<br /><br />DKOT puts you right back into the VH that simply rocks. I agree with many fans who have said DKOT could easily fit in the Fair Warning/Women and Children First era. BUT, the cool thing to me is that the album combines some elements of the past 35 years of VH with a whole bunch of new/unique elements that make it equally contemporary-sounding in many respects. The whole album is a musical paradox, from outside the box tracks like Bullethead and Honeybabysweetiedoll, to Big River, a re-written version of Big Trouble, a previously unreleased vintage VH track from the Simmons demo recording. Even though the whole song and solo closely mirror the original track they recorded decades ago, EVH's solo sounds as good or better than any other rock guitar contemporary alive. Eddie still uses lots of phaser and probably more wah than ever throughout many chorus and solos, plus some great new sounds/techniques here and there to mix things up. I always loved Alex's drums and felt he's been underrated because of his brother's acclaim; how about some credit for helping hold the band together and keeping the beat to one of the best guitarists ever? Wolfie is awesome too - he can even do back-up vocals and most people just bash him because he's not Eddie Van Halen or Michael Anthony, not because he can't play great bass guitar. And I think WVH's presence and influence definitely seem to have helped shake up things with the band's music. I had some doubts when Dave first resurfaced on/off again with VH, but he sounds great and he is such a creative lyricist. If you're just a casual VH fan, or don't really pay attention to the lyrics, you're missing some classic DLR poetry. Also, great editing and production of the album IMO with a variety of recording styles heard throughout the various tracks.<br /><br />And one final comment for all the VH vs. Chickenfoot debaters. I still like Hagar, Anthony, Satriani and Smith, individually and as a band. And maybe Sammy is right that individually, each member of CF is a technically superior musician in some respects.
